<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of mungbean and insights into evolution within Vigna species"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2014 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-11-11"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Mungbean (Vigna radiata) is a fast-growing, warm-season legume crop that is primarily cultivated in developing countries of Asia. Here we construct a draft genome sequence of mungbean to facilitate genome research into the subgenus Ceratotropis, which includes several important dietary legumes in Asia, and to enable a better understanding of the evolution of leguminous species. Based on the de novo assembly of additional wild mungbean species, the divergence of what was eventually domesticated and the sampled wild mungbean species appears to have predated domestication. Moreover, the de novo assembly of a tetraploid Vigna species (V. reflexo-pilosa var. glabra) provides genomic evidence of a recent allopolyploid event. The species tree is constructed using de novo RNA-seq assemblies of 22 accessions of 18 Vigna species and protein sets of Glycine max. The present assembly of V. radiata var. radiata will facilitate genome research and accelerate molecular breeding of the subgenus Ceratotropis. Mungbean is a fast-growing and warm-season legume crop, cultivated mainly in Asia. Here, the authors sequence the genomes of both wild and domesticated mungbean varieties and, together with detailed transcriptome data, provide insight into mungbean domestication, polyploidization and speciation.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Mungbean (<i>Vigna radiata</i>) is a fast-growing, warm-season legume crop that is primarily cultivated in developing countries of Asia. Here we construct a draft genome sequence of mungbean to facilitate genome research into the subgenus <i>Ceratotropis</i>, which includes several important dietary legumes in Asia, and to enable a better understanding of the evolution of leguminous species. Based on the <i>de novo</i> assembly of additional wild mungbean species, the divergence of what was eventually domesticated and the sampled wild mungbean species appears to have predated domestication. Moreover, the <i>de novo</i> assembly of a tetraploid <i>Vigna</i> species (<i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>) provides genomic evidence of a recent allopolyploid event. The species tree is constructed using <i>de novo</i> RNA-seq assemblies of 22 accessions of 18 <i>Vigna</i> species and protein sets of <i>Glycine max</i>. The present assembly of <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> will facilitate genome research and accelerate molecular breeding of the subgenus <i>Ceratotropis</i>.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Mungbean (<i>Vigna radiata</i> (L.) R. Wilczek) is a fast-growing warm-season legume species belonging to the papilionoid subfamily of the Fabaceae and has a diploid chromosome number of 2<i>n</i>=2<i>x</i>=22. Mungbean is cultivated mostly in South, East and Southeast Asia by small holder farmers for its edible seeds and sprouts. Mungbean seeds are a good source of dietary protein and contain higher levels of folate and iron than most other legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Keatinge, J. D. H., Easdown, W. J., Yang, R. Y., Chadha, M. L. &amp; Shanmugasundaram, S. Overcoming chronic malnutrition in a future warming world: the key importance of mungbean and vegetable soybean. Euphytica 180, 129â141 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d12427e958">1</a></sup>. Moreover, mungbean, as a legume crop, fixes atmospheric nitrogen via root rhizobial symbiosis, leading to improved soil fertility and texture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Graham, P. H. &amp; Vance, C. P. Legumes: importance and constraints to greater use. Plant Physiol. 131, 872â877 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12427e962">2</a></sup>. Intercropping mungbean in riceârice and riceâwheat systems increases the yield of the subsequent cereal crop and reduces pest incidence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Yaqub, M., Mahmood, T., Akhtar, M., Iqbal, M. M. &amp; Ali, S. Induction of mungbean [Vigna Radiata (L.) Wilczek] as a grain legume in the annual rice-wheat double cropping system. Pak. J. Bot. 42, 3125â3135 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d12427e967">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Defaria, S. M., Lewis, G. P., Sprent, J. I. &amp; Sutherland, J. M. Occurrence of nodulation in the Leguminosae. New Phytol. 111, 607â619 (1989)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12427e970">4</a></sup>. Genetic diversity data and archaeological evidence suggest that mungbean was domesticated in India<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fuller, D. Q. Contrasting patterns in crop domestication and domestication rates: recent archaeobotanical insights from the old world. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 100, 903â924 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12427e974">5</a></sup>. India is also the worldâs largest producer of mungbean, accounting for over 50% of the global annual production (~6 millionâtons), followed by China and Myanmar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Nair, R. et al. Genetic improvement of mungbean. SABRAO J. Breed. Genet. 44, 177â190 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d12427e978">6</a></sup>. In spite of its economic importance genomics of mungbean has not been intensively studied. Whole-genome sequences have become available for several legumes, such as <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, <i>Cicer arietinum</i>, <i>Lotus japonicus</i>, <i>Glycine max</i> and <i>Cajanus cajan</i>, whereas the genomic resources for mungbean remain scarce<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 240â246 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12427e998">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83â89 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1001">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520â524 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1004">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1007">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227â239 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1010">11</a></sup>.</p><p>Along with mungbean, the subgenus Ceratotropis of genus <i>Vigna</i> contains several major agriculturally important legumes, including crÃ©ole bean (<i>V. reflexo</i>-<i>pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>), black gram (<i>V. mungo</i>), rice bean (<i>V. umbellata</i>), moth bean (<i>V. aconitifolia</i>) and adzuki bean (<i>V. angularis</i>). The genome sizes of <i>Vigna</i> species are highly variable, ranging from 416 to 1,394âMb (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Parida, A., Raina, S. &amp; Narayan, R. Quantitative DNA variation between and within chromosome complements of Vigna species (Fabaceae). Genetica 82, 125â133 (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1044">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lakhanpaul, S. &amp; Babu, C. in Symposium on Grain Legumes 47â57 (New Delhi, India, 1991)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1047">13</a>). Most <i>Vigna</i> species are diploid, whereas <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> is tetraploid (2<i>n</i>=4<i>x</i>=44; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Egawa, Y. &amp; Tomooka, N. in JIRCAS International Symposium Series 2 112â120 (Tsukuba, Japan, 1991)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1063">14</a>). Genome expansion and polyploidization are considered major mechanisms of plant speciation, but the effects of polyploidy on species evolution still remain unclear<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Soltis, D. E., Buggs, R. J., Doyle, J. J. &amp; Soltis, P. S. What we still don't know about polyploidy. Taxon 59, 1387â1403 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1067">15</a></sup>. With the availability of modern genomics tools, traces of allopolyploidization can be tracked down which will further provide insights into adaption and speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Madlung, A. Polyploidy and its effect on evolutionary success: old questions revisited with new tools. Heredity 110, 99â104 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1072">16</a></sup>.</p><p>In the present study, we construct a draft genome of the cultivated mungbean (<i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> <i>VC1973A</i>) on a chromosomal scale. For detailed understanding of domestication, polyploidization and speciation in the genus <i>Vigna</i>, whole-genome sequences of a wild relative mungbean (<i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i>) and of a tetraploid relative of mungbean (<i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>), as well as transcriptome sequences of 22 <i>Vigna</i> accessions of 18 species are produced. Because of its short life cycle and small genome size, <i>Vigna</i> species may be used as model legume plants in genetic research to shed light on crop domestication and species divergence. Most importantly, the mungbean whole-genome sequence information produced by this study will boost genomics research in <i>Vigna</i> species and accelerate mungbean breeding programmes, which can be a potential framework for future resequencing efforts of the <i>Vigna</i> germplasms.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p>We sequenced domesticated <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> (2<i>n</i>=2<i>x</i>=22), its polyploid relative <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i> (2<i>n</i>=4<i>x</i>=44), and its wild relative <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i> (2<i>n</i>=2<i>x</i>=22). For <i>V</i>. <i>radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i>, the pure line <i>VC1973A</i> was chosen for genome sequencing. <i>VC1973A</i> was developed at the AVRDC-The World Vegetable Center in 1982; since then, it has been widely grown in Korea, Thailand, Taiwan, Canada and China as a heteronymous cultivar called âSeonhwanogduâ in Korea, âKamphaeng Saen 1â in Thailand and âZhong Luâ in China. A high-quality draft genome sequence of the diploid <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> <i>VC1973A</i> (2<i>n</i>=2<i>x</i>=22) with an estimated genome size of 579âMb (1.2âpg per 2C) was constructed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208â218 (1991)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1198">17</a></sup>. We prepared five libraries for sequencing by Illumina HiSeq2000 including 180 and 500âbp paired-end libraries and 5, 10 and 40âkb mate-pair libraries. These libraries provided a 320-fold base pair coverage of the estimated genome size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 1</a>). In addition, long reads providing approximately fivefold genome coverage were produced by sequencing using GS FLX+. The short reads were assembled using ALLPATHS-LG software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513â1518 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1206">18</a></sup>, producing 2,800 scaffolds with an N50 length of 1,507âkb. The total length of the scaffolds was ~431âMb. The long reads generated by GS FLX+ were assembled into 180,372 contigs using Newbler 2.5.3 software. In total, 144,213 of the GS FLX contigs were consistent with the scaffolds from ALLPATHS-LG. The non-matched GS FLX+ contigs were divided into 5âkb pseudo-mate-pair reads and assembled using ALLPATHS-LG software to improve the quality of the assembly, resulting in 2,748 scaffolds with an N50 length of 1.52âMb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The total length of the produced scaffolds was 431âMb, representing 80% of the genome size of 543âMb estimated from 25-base kmer frequency distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 3</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Summary of the de novo genome assembly and sequencing analysis of mungbean."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Summary of the <i>de novo</i> genome assembly and sequencing analysis of mungbean.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6443/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6443/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6443_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6443/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6443_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="508" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) SNP distributions between domesticated and wild mungbeans are depicted in the outer circle of the circular map. The middle circle represents the proportions of repeated elements including LTR/Gypsy (orange), LTR/Copia (yellow), LINE (blue) and DNA transposons (green). The inner circle shows gene densities across the chromosomes. On Vr05, two QTL locations were identified at the outermost layer, and they were consistent with <i>G. max</i> QTLs based on the syntenic relationship, as described schematically. (<b>b</b>) An OrthoMCL clustering analysis of five gene sets from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>V. radiata</i>. Each value within Venn diagram shows the number of orthologue/paralogue clusters. (<b>c</b>) Frequency distribution of Ks values in <i>V. radiata</i> (red), <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> (sky blue) and <i>G. max</i> (green).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6443/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We constructed a mungbean genetic map from a F<sub>6</sub> population of 190 recombinant inbred lines (RILs) generated by single-seed descent from a cross between <i>VC1973A</i> and the Korean landrace <i>V2984</i> (<i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i>) through genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS). Of 1,993 single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), 1,321 (covering 11 linkage groups) were used to construct a genetic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). In total, 239 scaffolds could be anchored to the genetic map through these SNPs; however, 86 scaffolds remained unoriented because only a single SNP was available to anchor these scaffolds on the genetic map. The resulting pseudochromosomes representing 11 linkage groups had an N50 length of 35.4âMb and covered 314âMb, corresponding to 73% of the total assembled sequences.</p><p>In addition to <i>VC1973A</i>, we sequenced a wild relative (<i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i>, accession <i>TC1966</i>) of domesticated <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i>. Two types of libraries, a 180âbp paired-end and a 5âkb mate-pair library, produced 8,161 scaffolds with an N50 length of 214âkb, covering 423âMb and corresponding to ~84% of the estimated genome size of 501âMb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 3</a>). A tetraploid relative, <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>, accession V1160, was also sequenced using 180âbp pair-end and 5âkb mate-pair libraries. The resulting assembly consisted of 29,166 scaffolds with a N50 length of 63âkb, covering 792âMb. The distribution of the kmer frequency led to an estimated genome size of 968âMb, which was almost twice the size of the mungbean genome. Thus, ~82% of the whole genome of the tetraploid <i>Vigna</i> species was captured by the sequencing effort.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repetitive sequences and transposable elements</h3><p>In plants, transposable elements are a major driver of genome expansion. Homology- and structure-based surveys have revealed that repetitive sequences occupy ~50.1% of the mungbean genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons are the predominant class of transposable elements in the mungbean genome, consistent with other legume species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 240â246 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1351">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83â89 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1354">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520â524 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1357">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1360">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227â239 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1363">11</a></sup>. We determined that 25.2% of the mungbean genome consisted of LTR/Gypsy, and 11.3% consisted of LTR/Copia type elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 4</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics Chapter 4,Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1370">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002â7013 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1373">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1376">21</a></sup>. In contrast, class II DNA transposons, including CACTA, Mutator, PIF-Harbinger, hAT, Helitron, MULE-MuDR and Tc1-Mariner, accounted for ~2.5% of the mungbean genome. The proportion of Mutator was the highest (1.4%), and Tc1-Mariner was the lowest (0.02%).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome characterization and gene annotation</h3><p>Genes were predicted and annotated from the repeat-masked mungbean genome sequence. We performed <i>de novo</i> and homology-based gene predictions using the Maker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188â196 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1391">22</a></sup> pipeline based on data from the RNA-seq assemblies of four different tissues: leaf, flower, pod and root (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Tables 5 and 6</a>). The predicted mungbean proteins represented a 97% match to the set of 248 eukaryotic core proteins proposed to assess the completeness of the genome sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 3</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genornes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1401">23</a></sup>. The sequence length distributions of the genes, exons, coding DNA sequences (CDS) and introns by plant species showed high consistency among <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, soybean and mungbean, indicating that our gene predictions were highly reliable (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). In total, 22,427 genes were identified with high confidence, and 18,378 genes were located on pseudochromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). The 22,427 protein sets of <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> and the protein sequences of <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> and <i>G. max</i> were compared by software OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1434">24</a></sup>. There were a total of 6,799 gene clusters shared in all five species, and 160 clusters were composed of only <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). We assigned annotations to these proteins using Interproscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Mulder, N. J. et al. InterPro: an integrated documentation resource for protein families, domains and functional sites. Brief Bioinform. 3, 225â235 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1448">25</a></sup> and BLAST against <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Data 1</a>). In addition, we predicted 2,310 non-coding genes, including 629 transfer RNAs, 280 ribosomal RNAs, 537 microRNAs, 717 small nucleolar RNAs, 110 small nuclear RNAs and 37 other regulatory RNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><p>In total, 1,850 genes encoding transcription factors (TFs) were identified in the mungbean genome by Pfam annotation, and the relative TF abundance was compared with that of other plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 8</a>). The overall distribution of TF genes in each genome was highly consistent among the plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). The most highly represented TF family was MYB, followed by AP2/EREBP and bHLH. Notably, the bZIP2 family accounted for &lt;1% of the total TFs in legume genomes compared with those of non-legume genomes, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Zea mays</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>Brachypodium distachyon,</i> in which bZIP2 represented &gt;3%. Thus there was most likely a reduction in this specific TF family in a common ancestor of these legume genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Domestication of mungbean</h3><p>Crops have undergone domestication through selective breeding to acquire traits that are beneficial for their use by humans. The relationship between the genomes of <i>VC1973A</i> and its wild relative (<i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i>) <i>TC1966</i> can serve as a model to understand mungbean domestication. The paired-end short reads derived from <i>TC1966</i> and an additional domesticated landrace, <i>V2984</i>, were mapped against the <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The mapped regions spanned 401 and 422âMb, representing 93 and 98% coverage of the <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> genome sequence, respectively. In total, 2,922,833 SNPs supported by at least five reads were found between domesticated and wild mungbean, corresponding to a SNP frequency of 6.78 per 1âkb. Of the 63,294 SNPs detected in the CDS regions, 30,405 were non-synonymous, accounting for a total of 10,641 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Out of 342,853 total INDELs, 55,689 were located within the genic boundaries, including 576 deletions and 526 insertions that were predicted to cause frameshift mutations in 551 and 506 genes, respectively. Among the domesticated mungbeans, 775,831 high-confidence SNPs at a frequency of 1.8 SNPs per 1âkb, including 98,590 INDELs, were identified. Of the total 19,541 SNPs in the CDS regions, 9,378 were found to be non-synonymous and affected a total of 3,233 genes.</p><p>In total, 235,641,385 bases were conserved among all three genotypes. There were 2,425,069 bases conserved between the domesticated mungbeans, which were exclusively polymorphic between <i>VC1973A</i> and <i>TC1966</i>. Of the 51,351 wild-genotype-specific mungbean SNPs in CDS, 24,599 were non-synonymous where they were distributed over 9,344 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). Any protein changes underlying phenotypic differences between domesticated and wild mungbean should be among these non-synonymous SNPs.</p><p>Based on the <i>de novo</i> assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 3</a>), the genome-wide alignment of these scaffolds revealed considerable consistency in the overall genome organization between domesticated mungbean and its wild relative (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). However, there was some degree (80â95%) of alignment block differentiation between wild and domesticated mungbean (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). With 18,981 genes having a collinear relationship between the wild and domesticated mungbean, there was a recent peak of Ks frequency of the synteny blocks having a modal value at 0.01 (modal age of 1 million years ago (MYA)) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 10</a>).</p><p>The Ks values between <i>VC1973A</i> and <i>V2984</i> were calculated using SNPs in coding sequences of <i>V2984</i>, and consequently, its modal age was 1 MYA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 10</a>). This is similar to the modal age between <i>VC1973A</i> and <i>TC1966</i>, suggesting that allelic differences between cultivated and wild mungbean are similar to differences between cultivated mungbeans.</p><p>Repetitive elements are known to be a major driving force behind plant evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lisch, D. How important are transposons for plant evolution? Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 49â61 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1585">26</a></sup>. A higher proportion of repetitive elements is found in domesticated (50.1%) than in wild (46.9%) accession. While the other TEs were distributed equally in both species, Gypsy elements were more widely dispersed in domesticated mungbean accession (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Duplication history of legume genomes</h3><p>The subfamily Papilionoideae contains the majority of legume crops and the major model legume species, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>L. japonicus</i>. Members of this family shared an ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) event ~58 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 240â246 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1607">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83â89 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1610">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520â524 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1613">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1616">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227â239 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1619">11</a></sup> before the family split into several major groups, the two largest being the warm-season millettioid and the cool-season Hologalegina clades, ~54 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. Evolutionary rates analysis of leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575â594 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1623">27</a></sup>. Most of the legume crops within the millettioid clade, such as <i>C. cajan</i> and <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i>, underwent no additional WGD events. However, the soybean genome underwent another round of WGD ~5â13 MYA, which resulted in its high chromosome number (2<i>n</i>=4<i>x</i>=40) and an increase of its genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1640">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Egan, A. N. Dating the origins of polyploidy events. New Phytol. 186, 73â85 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1643">28</a></sup>. Comparison of 2,917 pairs of paralogous genes residing in duplicated collinear blocks within the mungbean genome revealed that the mungbean has experienced only one ancient WGD. There was a single major peak of Ks frequency of the synteny blocks with a modal value at 0.61 (modal age of 59 MYA), which is near the origin of the Papilionoideae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 10</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. Evolutionary rates analysis of leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575â594 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1654">27</a></sup>. In contrast, a recent peak at the Ks value of 0.07 (6.8 MYA) was detected from a pairwise comparison of homologues with no supporting collinearity of the genes, possibly because of recent small-scale duplications including tandem and ectopic duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Freeling, M. Bias in plant gene content following different sorts of duplication: tandem, whole-genome, segmental, or by transposition. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 60, 433â453 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1658">29</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). Especially, tandem duplicates, which were searched by the homologous gene pairs located within 10 consecutive genes on the same chromosome, increased ~7â13 MYA, generating 252 tandem gene clusters with Ks peaks of ~0.06â0.12 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). The tandem duplicates were enriched for the following gene ontology category terms: âdefence responseâ, âcell wall modificationâ, âsecondary metabolic processâ, âsulphate transportâ, ârecognition of pollenâ, âtransmembrane transportâ and âprotein amino acid phosphorylationâ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><p>To understand more recent allopolyploidy in <i>Vigna</i>, we sequenced the genome of <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>, which is known to be an allotetraploid (2<i>n</i>=4<i>x</i>=44; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tomooka, N., Vaughan, D. &amp; Moss, H. The Asian Vigna: genus Vigna subgenus Ceratotropis genetic resources Springer (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1689">30</a>). In total, 41,844 genes, almost twice the number of mungbean genes, were predicted based on <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>G. max</i> proteins, in addition to the leaf transcriptome sequence of <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>. The synteny blocks of <i>V</i>. <i>reflexo</i>-<i>pilosa</i> exhibited a recent peak with Ks values having a modal value at 0.07 (modal age of 6.8 MYA) and weak ancient traces consistent with the shared papilionoid WGD seen more clearly in <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> and <i>G. max</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). Assuming that <i>V</i>. <i>reflexo</i>-<i>pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i> is allopolyploid, the divergence time of the donor species of the allopolyploid genome was estimated at 6.8 MYA.</p><p>The genome comparison of <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> with <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. arietinum</i>, <i>C. cajan</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and <i>M. truncatula</i> revealed the presence of well-conserved macrosynteny blocks, although these blocks were highly dispersed among plant species with different numbers of chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Given the closer relationship of <i>Vigna</i> to <i>Glycine</i>, most of the <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> genes were found in genomic regions with synteny to <i>G. max</i>. Of the 18,378 genes on pseudochromosomes, 14,569 were located in 1,059 synteny blocks of orthologues or paralogues, which were used to determine the time of divergence between <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>G. max</i>. The frequency of median Ks of synteny blocks showed a peak with a modal value at 0.29 (modal age of 28 MYA; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig2">Fig. 2aâc</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 10</a>). The non-collinear 3,807 genes on pseudomolecules, which may have been fractionated after the ancient WGD or duplicated at a small scale, were mostly enriched in the gene ontology categories of âdefence responseâ and âtranslationâ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Comparisons of the estimated divergence times based on the peak Ks values showed that there was greater divergence between <i>Vigna</i> and <i>Cajanus</i> than between <i>Vigna</i> and <i>Glycine</i>, as expected. There were 11,853 mungbean genes in synteny with the <i>C. cajan</i> genome. The frequency of median Ks of synteny blocks showed the main peak having a modal value at 0.32 (modal age of 31 MYA) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig2">Fig. 2a,b,d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Divergence times estimated here were consistently larger than comparable estimates from chloroplast genes or non-genic regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. Evolutionary rates analysis of leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575â594 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1828">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Stefanovic, S., Pfeil, B. E., Palmer, J. D. &amp; Doyle, J. J. Relationships among phaseoloid legumes based on sequences from eight chloroplast regions. Syst. Bot. 34, 115â128 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1831">31</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Analysis of syntenic relationships in legumes within the millettioid clade."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Analysis of syntenic relationships in legumes within the millettioid clade.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6443/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6443/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6443_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6443/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6443_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="464" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Estimation of divergence times between <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>C. cajan</i>, and between <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>G. max</i>. (<b>b</b>) Visualization of chromosomal rearrangements among <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i>, <i>G. max</i> and <i>C. cajan</i>. Chromosomes among <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i>, <i>C. cajan</i> and <i>G. max</i> (A genome) are connected by red lines, and chromosomes between <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> and <i>G. max</i> (B genome) are linked by blue lines. (<b>c</b>) Syntenic relationship between <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>G. max</i>; the <i>x</i>-axis indicates chromosomal locations and the <i>y</i>-axis indicates Ks value. Each dot represents the location of a synteny block with its Ks median value, showing both conservation and chromosomal rearrangements of syntenic blocks. (<b>d</b>) Depiction of the syntenic relationship between <i>V. radiata</i> and <i>C. cajan</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6443/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Vigna speciation based on transcriptome analysis</h3><p>Speciation and domestication have involved substantial adaptations to various climates and cultural environments. Asian <i>Vigna</i> species (subgenus Ceratotropis) are morphologically and physiologically diverse, consistent with their distribution across South, Southeast and East Asia, extending from tropical regions to the Himalayan highlands<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Javadi, F., Tun, Y. T., Kawase, M., Guan, K. Y. &amp; Yamaguchi, H. Molecular phylogeny of the subgenus Ceratotropis (genus Vigna, Leguminosae) reveals three eco-geographical groups and Late Pliocene-Pleistocene diversification: evidence from four plastid DNA region sequences. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 108, 367â380 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1945">32</a></sup>. Subgenus Ceratotropis has been divided into three taxonomic groups (Angulares, Ceratotropis and Aconitifoliae) based on its morphological characters such as seedling germination, floral size and growth habit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tomooka, N., Vaughan, D. &amp; Moss, H. The Asian Vigna: genus Vigna subgenus Ceratotropis genetic resources Springer (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1949">30</a></sup>. From the <i>de novo</i> assembly of RNA-seq from leaf tissues of 22 accessions of 16 Asian <i>Vigna</i> species, representing each of these groups, as well as 1 African <i>Vigna</i> species (<i>V. subterranea</i>) and 1 Eurasian <i>Vigna</i> species (<i>V. vexillata</i>), we identified 1,121 shared orthologous loci using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1972">24</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Tables 11 and 12</a>). Two phylogenetic analyses were conducted. (1) A Bayesian multispecies coalescent analysis (*BEAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Heled, J. &amp; Drummond, A. J. Bayesian inference of species trees from multilocus data. Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 570â580 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d12427e1980">33</a></sup>) used 9 orthologous loci from 20 diploid <i>Vigna</i> species and the two homoeologous genomes of the polyploid, <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> as separate operational taxonomic units (OTU) based on the within-genome synteny relationship and the inter-genome synteny relationships with <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i>. This time-calibrated analysis also used the two reconstructed homoeologous genomes of <i>G. max</i> as outgroups and for calibration based on the ca. 19 MYA divergence of <i>Vigna</i> and <i>Glycine</i> estimated from chloroplast gene phylogenies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. Evolutionary rates analysis of leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575â594 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2006">27</a></sup>. (2) A maximum likelihood (ML)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307â321 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2010">34</a></sup> analysis used 375 concatenated orthologous loci from 20 diploid <i>Vigna</i> accessions.</p><p>The *BEAST and ML analyses both identified two clades with good support (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>), in agreement with a previously published chloroplast phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Javadi, F., Tun, Y. T., Kawase, M., Guan, K. Y. &amp; Yamaguchi, H. Molecular phylogeny of the subgenus Ceratotropis (genus Vigna, Leguminosae) reveals three eco-geographical groups and Late Pliocene-Pleistocene diversification: evidence from four plastid DNA region sequences. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 108, 367â380 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2026">32</a></sup>. The *BEAST and ML trees both placed <i>V. subramaniana</i> (which was not in the chloroplast analysis) as sister to <i>V. radiata.</i> Although there were several differences in some other clades appeared in all three phylogenies, which may be due to different sampling, the *BEAST analysis allowed relationships of the two homoeologous genomes of <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> to be traced. One was found to be closely related to diploid <i>V. trinervia</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>), in agreement with previous chloroplast results<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Javadi, F., Tun, Y. T., Kawase, M., Guan, K. Y. &amp; Yamaguchi, H. Molecular phylogeny of the subgenus Ceratotropis (genus Vigna, Leguminosae) reveals three eco-geographical groups and Late Pliocene-Pleistocene diversification: evidence from four plastid DNA region sequences. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 108, 367â380 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2046">32</a></sup> and suggesting that <i>V. trinervia</i> or a close ally was the maternal progenitor of the allopolyploid. A maximum date for the polyploidy event of 0.09 MYA is given by the divergence between these two OTUs. The second homoeologous genome was found to be sister to the entire second diploid clade comprising the <i>Angulares</i> group; this topology and the much older divergence date (2.7 MYA) suggests that the diploid progenitor lineage has not been sampled, and may be extinct.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Species tree for 22 Vigna accessions and close legume relative, G. max."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Species tree for 22 <i>Vigna</i> accessions and close legume relative, <i>G. max</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6443/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6443/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6443_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6443/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6443_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="404" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Average divergence dates were depicted for the nodes estimated by a Bayesian MCMC method. The horizontal bars represent the 95% highest posterior density (HPD) interval at each node estimated by tested genes. The colour of the square on each node represents the posterior probability according to colour gradation from black (0) through blue (0.5) to red (1). The root divergence time was set to a 19 MYA between <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> and <i>G. max</i> following the estimation of Lavin <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. Evolutionary rates analysis of leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575â594 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2087">27</a></sup></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6443/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genomic resources for mungbean breeding</h3><p>The development of molecular markers is critical for crop improvement programmes. Although molecular marker resources are limited for mungbean, there have been several efforts to identify the genomic regions related to domestication-related traits, including seed size and seed germination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Isemura, et al. Construction of a genetic linkage map and genetic analysis of domestication related triats in mungbean (Vigna radiata). PLoS ONE 7, e41304 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2106">35</a></sup>. Moreover, molecular markers are important for integrating useful alleles of wild mungbean, such as bruchid resistance, into domesticated mungbean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Chen, H. M. et al. The major quantitative trait locus for mungbean yellow mosaic Indian virus resistance is tightly linked in repulsion phase to the major bruchid resistance locus in a cross between mungbean [Vigna radiata (L.) Wilczek] and its wild relative Vigna radiata ssp sublobata. Euphytica 192, 205â216 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2110">36</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). With our whole-genome sequencing and gene content data, a syntenic relationship was revealed by a comparative analysis with well-characterized <i>G. max</i> quantitative trait locus (QTLs) to provide important clues for the identification of mungbean QTLs. The synteny blocks of seed size/germination and bruchid resistance QTL regions matched the soybean synteny blocks containing simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers linked to seed weight and nematode resistance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). Hence, our resequencing-derived wild-genotype-specific mungbean SNPs and the comparative genomic information may further facilitate a variety of molecular breeding activities and will ultimately assist the identification of the responsible genes for the corresponding traits.</p><p>We also developed SSR markers using MISA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Thiel, T., Michalek, W., Varshney, R. K. &amp; Graner, A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 106, 411â422 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2126">37</a></sup>, resulting in the identification of 200,808 SSRs from 1,544 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 13</a>). The number of tri-repeat unit SSRs, which were efficiently used for genotyping, was 17,898.</p><p>The identification of molecular markers for the resistance against biotic and abiotic stresses is crucial. As previously reported, most resistance genes encode proteins with two core domains: nucleotide-binding sites (NBSs) and leucine-rich repeats (LRRs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Meyers, B. C., Kozik, A., Griego, A., Kuang, H. H. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 15, 809â834 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2136">38</a></sup>. A hidden Markov model (HMM) was established for each domain after a Pfam domain search. In mungbean, we found 73 LRR genes with NB-ARC domains, in which all of the NBS-LRR genes exhibited homology with known disease resistance genes in the UniProt database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Consortium, U. Reorganizing the protein space at the Universal Protein Resource (UniProt). Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D71âD75 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2140">39</a></sup>. In addition, 19 of 464 LRR genes without NB-ARC domains were identified as genes for disease resistance and damage repair (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 14</a>). Our data revealed 30 SNP markers flanking resistance genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 15</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>We constructed 421âMb (80%) of the total estimated <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> genome and identified 22,427 high-confidence protein-coding genes and 160 <i>Vigna</i> gene clusters. This is the first draft genome sequence within the genus <i>Vigna</i>. Together with the genome and transcriptome sequences of other <i>Vigna</i> species produced by this study, it will facilitate further genome research into the subgenus Ceratotropis.</p><p>Genomic sequencing provided insights into the history of polyploidy in papilionoid legumes and within the genus <i>Vigna</i>. The signature of the ancient whole-genome duplication shared among papilionoid legumes such as <i>Medicago</i>, <i>Lotus</i>, <i>Glycine</i> and <i>Cajanus</i>â a peak in the age distribution of pairs of duplicated genesâwas observed in Vigna species. The date of 59 MYA estimated from this distribution was consistent with previous estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Schlueter, J. A. et al. Mining EST databases to resolve evolutionary events in major crop species. Genome 47, 868â876 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2193">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell 16, 1667â1678 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2196">41</a></sup>. Bioinformatic separation of the allotetraploid (2<i>n</i>=44) genome of <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i> into its constituent homoeologous subgenomes and their inclusion as separate taxa (A and B genomes) in a Bayesian species tree phylogeny allowed us to identify and date its origins. The allopolyploidy event occurred at maximum date of 0.09 MYA, with one diploid genome and the chloroplast genome donated by a close relative of <i>V. trinervia</i> and the second diploid genome from an unsampled member of a clade that included several species, among them <i>V. minima</i>, to which it has been considered to be related (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tomooka, N., Vaughan, D. &amp; Moss, H. The Asian Vigna: genus Vigna subgenus Ceratotropis genetic resources Springer (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2219">30</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Schematic illustration of allopolyploidization history of the V. reflexo-pilosa var. glabra."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Schematic illustration of allopolyploidization history of the <i>V. reflexo</i>-<i>pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6443/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6443/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6443_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6443/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6443_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="274" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Red arrow indicates the putative date (maximum 0.09 MYA) of polyploidization event of <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>. The number at each node represents the divergence time estimated by a Bayesian MCMC method.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6443/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It has been suggested that the domestication and cultivation of mungbean was initiated in the northwest and far south of India 4,000â6,000 years ago, based on the geographical distribution of wild mungbean and archaeological records from India<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fuller, D. Q. Contrasting patterns in crop domestication and domestication rates: recent archaeobotanical insights from the old world. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 100, 903â924 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2262">5</a></sup>. The domesticated mungbean is considered to have spread mainly throughout Southeast Asia and East Asia from India via different routes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Tomooka, N., Lairungreang, C., Nakeeraks, P., Egawa, Y. &amp; Thavarasook, C. Center of genetic diversity and dissemination pathways in mungbean deduced from seed protein electrophoresis. Theor. Appl. Genet. 83, 289â293 (1992)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2266">42</a></sup>. It is possible that the domesticated mungbean was imported from India to China via the Silk Road and subsequently spread to Southeast Asia. As we sampled only one accession of <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i> in our study, we could not observe any population substructure in <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i>, and we thus cannot determine whether there are <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i> lineages more closely related to cultivated mungbean than the one we sampled, nor could we obtain evidence for multiple origins of the crop variety. Based on the Ks distribution, the divergence between the <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> and <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i> lineages sampled here and also between the <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> (<i>VC1973A</i>) and <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> (<i>V2984</i>) occurred ~1 MYA, substantially predating domestication of mungbean (4,000â6,000 years ago) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Similar findings about the divergence of wild lineages and their cultivated derivatives have been reported in other cultivated species, such as rice and soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kim, M. Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2324">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Gross, B. L. &amp; Olsen, K. M. Genetic perspectives on crop domestication. Trends. Plant Sci. 15, 529â537 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2327">44</a></sup>.</p><p>Mungbean is grown mostly in developing countries, which has delayed fundamental genome research. To date, because of the lack of genome sequence data for <i>Vigna</i> species, molecular breeding has not yet been fully implemented. The whole-genome sequence and the high-density genetic map give access to efficient SNP discovery and thus will boost genomics-assisted selection for mungbean improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant materials</h3><p>Twenty-two accessions of 18 <i>Vigna</i> species were used in this study, including the Asian domesticated species of black gram, mungbean, adzuki bean, rice bean, crÃ©ole bean and moth bean, as well as the African domesticated species. In addition to those domesticated species, we combined the wild progenitors of black gram, mungbean, rice bean and crÃ©ole bean. Instead of the wild progenitor of adzuki bean (<i>V. angularis</i> var. nipponensis), <i>V. nepalensis</i> was included in this study as a variant of <i>V. angularis</i> var. nipponensis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Tun, Y. T. &amp; Yamaguchi, H. Sequence variation of four chloroplast non-coding regions among wild, weedy and cultivated Vigna angularis accessions. Breed. Sci. 58, 325â330 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2361">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Tateishi, Y. &amp; Maxted, N. New species and combinations in Vigna subgenus Ceratotropis (Piper) Verdc.(Leguminosae, Phaseoleae). Kew Bull. 57, 625â633 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2364">46</a></sup>. All of the species belong to the subgenus Ceratotropis (Asian <i>Vigna</i>), with the exception of <i>V. subterranea</i> and <i>V. vexillata</i> which belongs to the subgenus <i>Vigna</i> (African <i>Vigna</i>) and <i>Plectotropis</i> (Eurasian <i>Vigna</i>), respectively. These accessions were collected from several national and international genebanks including Chai Nat Field Crops Research Center in Thailand, the National Agrobiodiversity Center in Korea, the National Institute of Agrobiological Sciences in Japan, the National Botanic Garden of Belgium in Belgium, the Australian Collections of Plant Genetic Resources in Australia, the International Center for Tropical Agriculture in Columbia, the International Livestock Research Institute in Kenya and the International Institute of Tropical Agriculture in Nigeria. These collected <i>Vigna</i> accessions have a diploid chromosome composition of 2<i>n</i>=2<i>x</i>=22, whereas <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> is an only allotetraploid <i>Vigna</i> species (2<i>n</i>=4<i>x</i>=44).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p>We sequenced the mungbean genome by two NGS platforms, Illumina Hiseq2000 and GS FLX+, with five libraries of a 180-bp fragment, 5, 10, 40-kb mate-pairs and one single linear library. For genome assembly of <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i> and <i>V. reflexo-pilosa</i> var. <i>glabra</i>, 180-bp fragment and 5-kb mate-pair libraries were sequenced by Illumina Hiseq2000. The reads produced by Illumina and GS FLX+ were assembled by using the software packages of ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513â1518 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2433">18</a></sup> and newbler, respectively. The Newbler contigs were used to validate the assemblies of ALLPATHS-LG using megablast with an <i>E</i>-value cut-off of 1eâ100. The not-matched and overlapped contigs were chopped into pseudo 5-kb mate-pair reads and then assembled again using ALLPATHS-LG.</p><p>SSR were predicted based on ALLPATHS-LG assembled scaffolds through the software MISA ( <a href="http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/misa.html">http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/misa.html</a>) with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Thiel, T., Michalek, W., Varshney, R. K. &amp; Graner, A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 106, 411â422 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6443#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d12427e2451">37</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">SNP/INDEL analysis and whole-genome alignment</h3><p>The short reads obtained from the <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>radiata</i> (<i>V2984</i>), Kyung-Ki Jaerae #5, and a <i>V. radiata</i> var. <i>sublobata</i> (<i>TC1966</i>),wild mungbean, were used for SNP/INDEL analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6443#MOESM795">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The mapping of total reads mapping was performed with NextGenMap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Sedlazeck, F. J., Rescheneder, P. &amp; von Haeseler, A. NextGenMap: fast and accurate read mapping in highly polymorphic genomes.
